Generated Hypothesis Portfolio

H2 primary score 0.495; inference inferred
The concentration of prizes among elites reduces the diversity of innovative ideas in science over time, as elite prizewinners' networks become insular and prioritize incremental work over radical novelty.
H1 secondary score 0.465; inference inferred
The innovation gap between prizewinners and nonprizewinners is driven by a selection effect: prizewinners possess pre-existing traits (e.g., risk-taking, cognitive flexibility) that cause both higher innovation and eventual prize receipt, rather than the prize itself causing sustained innovation.
Both selected hypotheses are shown on the front page; treat new ideas as audit targets, not proven facts.

Review Walkthrough

  • 1. Provide input: submit a paper PDF, paper URL, arXiv ID, or research guidance through the CLI or an optional chat wrapper.
  • 2. Run agent: the pipeline records every tool step and stores reusable memory.
  • 3. Open this page: explain the dashboard before drilling into details.
  • 4. Show audit logic: GREEN is direct support, YELLOW is partial/inferred, RED is failure or contradiction.

1. Run Summary

This section is the executive checksum for the run. It records the run ID, verifies the most important ordering constraint, and defines the report's audit colors. The key process guarantee is that literature search must occur before hypothesis generation, so the generated idea is grounded in retrieved papers rather than produced from the input PDF alone. Read GREEN/YELLOW/RED as claim-audit labels, not as paper-quality labels.

Run ID: 0725bbf5-c1c5-42f1-800a-48bd0ea9cde1

Literature search happened before hypothesis generation: YES.

GREEN labels require verified references, bibliographic score >= 0.75, direct support, evidence snippets, provenance, and a written reason. The score column in the verification table is bibliographic identity confidence, not support strength. YELLOW means uncertain, partial, or a novel claim that still needs experiment; RED means not found, unsupported, contradicted, or clearly wrong.

Direct evidenceReference identity is verified, evidence explicitly addresses the claim, and the reason explains the support relation.
Indirect evidenceEvidence is relevant background or motivation, but it does not directly prove the full claim.
Background / absentEvidence is too weak, abstract-only, missing, or only useful as context.
Contradictory evidenceEvidence conflicts with the claim or contains explicit contradiction cues.

Four Error Categories

Unsupported ClaimThe conclusion is not supported by any available input material.
OverclaimThe material supports a weaker conclusion, but the generated claim states a stronger one.
Mis-citationThe cited material is topic-related but does not support the specific claim.
ContradictionThe generated claim conflicts with the cited material.

Evidence Source Snapshot

Total evidence records: 35; input-paper evidence: 7; retrieved/external evidence: 28; abstract evidence ratio: 0.829.

2. Input Paper Profiles

This section shows the structured profile extracted from each submitted PDF before any external retrieval or hypothesis generation. A profile contains the detected title, authors, abstract, and high-level source claims. These fields are produced by deterministic PDF parsing and profiling heuristics, with optional LLM assistance only when enabled. They are not treated as final truth: if the title, abstract, author list, or key claims are wrong, later search queries, relatedness filtering, literature-map items, and citation audits should be interpreted cautiously.

Clean profileTitle, authors, abstract, and key claims match the PDF; downstream retrieval is likely aligned.
Partial profileSome fields are missing or noisy; search may still work, but map and hypothesis quality should be reviewed manually.
Bad profileTitle/abstract/key claims are visibly wrong; later sections may be answering the wrong paper and should be treated as unreliable.

PNAS 2025 Vol. 122 No. 40 e2424143122 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significance

Authors:

Abstract: Science prizes purportedly reward innovation and explorations of new phenomena. Yet in practice, prizes may inadvertently divert resources from similarly impactful but less celebrated scholars. Despite this paradox, and even as prizes proliferate, knowledge of how prizewinning relates to innovation is nascent. Analyzing 2,460 worldwide prizes, we compared the innovativeness of over 23,000 prizewinners and matched nonprizewinners whose performance records were statistically equivalent up to the prize year. First, we find that prizewinners are more innovative. Their research is more likely to combine existing ideas in new ways, integrate a topic’s historical and contemporary thinking, and incorporate interdisciplinary perspectives. Second, although prizewinners and matched nonprizewinners have statistically equivalent impact and productivity records up to the prize year, at about five years before the prize, prizewinners’ papers become more innovative than their matched peers. This difference widens each year, peaks during the prize year, and then persists for the remainder of their careers. Third, network embed- dedness predicts unusual innovativeness. Compared to nonprizewinners, prizewinners’ collaborations are shorter in duration, encompass wider exposure to unfamiliar topics, and involve coauthors whose networks minimally overlap with each other. The findings’ implications for innovation in science and the efficacy of reward systems and innovation in science are discussed.

Key claims:

  • Science prizes purportedly reward innovation and explorations of new phenomena.
  • Yet in practice, prizes may inadvertently divert resources from similarly impactful but less celebrated scholars.
  • Despite this paradox, and even as prizes proliferate, knowledge of how prizewinning relates to innovation is nascent.
  • Analyzing 2,460 worldwide prizes, we compared the innovativeness of over 23,000 prizewinners and matched nonprizewinners whose performance records were statistically equivalent up to the prize year.

3. Paper Index Legend

This section defines the shorthand paper labels used throughout the report. Labels such as P1, P2, ... refer to retrieved or connected papers after deduplication and relatedness filtering; the internal JSON index remains zero-based, so P1 corresponds to JSON index 0. The relation field explains why a paper entered the candidate pool, for example input seed, cited by the input paper, citing the input paper, semantic expansion, or topical search. The relatedness score is the gate score used before map and hypothesis generation; it is not a citation-support score.

Distance 0 / input seedThe submitted paper itself; useful as context but not enough to prove a new idea.
Distance 1-2Cited-by or citing-neighborhood papers; these are stronger candidates for connected literature.
Semantic expansionTitle/abstract-near papers added when citation graph is sparse; useful but weaker than citation-chain evidence.
Low relatednessRejected or marginal papers should not support hypotheses, even if they are real papers.

4. Literature Map Summary

This section turns the connected paper pool into a literature map. A thread is a cluster of papers that share a method, problem setting, or conceptual role. A gap is a missing test, boundary condition, mechanism, or application regime suggested by the connected evidence. A tension records an unresolved contrast, such as two papers making claims that are hard to compare directly or using incompatible assumptions. A cross-domain connection is a possible transfer of an idea from one setting to another. These items are generated from paper titles, abstracts, retrieved snippets, and evidence IDs; they are the immediate inputs to hypothesis generation, not final claims by themselves.

Thread onlyThe literature clusters cleanly, but may not yet imply a new research question.
Gap + evidenceA plausible place to generate a hypothesis; check evidence IDs before trusting it.
TensionA stronger idea source when assumptions, benchmarks, or claims do not line up across papers.
Empty tension/connectionNot a failure by itself; it means the retrieved literature did not expose a clear contrast or transfer opportunity.

Map generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned a valid literature map with at least one thread and one gap.

Threads

  • T1 Prizewinner innovation and network embeddedness: Prizewinners exhibit higher innovativeness (combinatorial, historical, interdisciplinary) and distinct collaboration patterns (shorter durations, diverse topics, low overlap) compared to matched nonprizewinners. This innovation gap emerges ~5 years before the prize and persists.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 5 Evidence 6 Evidence 7 Evidence 11 Evidence 23
  • T2 Prize network concentration and elite stratification: The prize network is highly concentrated among a small elite, with 64.1% of prizewinners winning multiple prizes, suggesting constrained knowledge pathways and cumulative advantage.
    Evidence: Evidence 14 Evidence 16 Evidence 17
  • T3 Mentorship and prizewinning success: Mentorship strongly predicts protégé success, including prizewinning. Mentors with a hidden capability for creating celebrated research transmit this to protégés, increasing protégés' likelihood of prizewinning 2-4x.
    Evidence: Evidence 24 Evidence 25 Evidence 27 Evidence 28
  • T4 Intellectual synthesis in mentorship and career success: Trainee success is predicted by the degree of intellectual synthesis between graduate and postdoctoral mentors, especially when mentors have disparate expertise.
    Evidence: Evidence 32 Evidence 34 Evidence 35

Gaps

  • G1: Causal effect of prizes on innovation vs. selection: P1 shows prizewinners are more innovative before the prize, but does not disentangle whether prizes cause sustained innovation or merely select for pre-existing innovative traits. No experimental or quasi-experimental evidence in the connected papers. Why it matters: Determines whether prize systems effectively incentivize innovation or merely reward pre-existing talent, with implications for prize design and resource allocation.
    Evidence: Evidence 6 Evidence 7
  • G2: Mechanisms linking network embeddedness to innovation: P1 and P3 show correlations between collaboration patterns (short duration, low overlap) and innovativeness, but do not identify causal mechanisms (e.g., knowledge diversity, reduced conformity pressure, access to novel information). Why it matters: Understanding mechanisms could inform interventions to boost innovation in nonprizewinners and early-career researchers.
    Evidence: Evidence 11 Evidence 23
  • G3: Interaction between mentorship and prize network effects: P5 shows mentorship boosts prizewinning odds, but no study examines how mentor network embeddedness (e.g., prize network position) affects protégé innovation patterns. P1's network measures are at individual level, not mentor-protégé dyad. Why it matters: Could reveal whether prizewinning is transmitted through network access or direct mentoring, guiding mentorship policies.
    Evidence: Evidence 27 Evidence 11
  • G4: Generalizability beyond STEM and biomedical fields: Connected papers focus on STEM (P5, P8) or broad science (P1-P4). No evidence from social sciences, humanities, or arts. Prize dynamics and innovation patterns may differ. Why it matters: Limits the scope of conclusions; cross-domain studies could test universality of findings.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 14 Evidence 26 Evidence 31
  • G5: Long-term effects of prize concentration on scientific diversity: P2 shows prize concentration among elites, but no study examines whether this concentration reduces diversity of ideas or demographics over time. P1 mentions resource diversion but does not test it. Why it matters: If prizes reinforce existing elites, they may hinder innovation from underrepresented groups or unconventional ideas.
    Evidence: Evidence 14 Evidence 16 Evidence 2

Tensions

  • X1: Prizes reward innovation vs. divert resources: P1 states prizes reward innovation but may divert resources from equally impactful nonprizewinners. This tension is acknowledged but not resolved.
    Evidence: Evidence 1 Evidence 2
  • X2: Prize network concentration vs. innovation breadth: P2 finds prizes concentrated among elites, suggesting constrained ideas, while P1 finds prizewinners are more innovative. This implies elite concentration may not stifle innovation among winners but could limit overall field innovation.
    Evidence: Evidence 14 Evidence 5
  • X3: Mentorship transmission of prizewinning skill vs. individual network effects: P5 emphasizes mentor's hidden capability, while P1 emphasizes prizewinners' own network embeddedness. It is unclear whether mentorship or network access is more critical for innovation.
    Evidence: Evidence 25 Evidence 11

Cross-Domain Connections

  • C1: Network embeddedness measures from science of science (P1) applied to organizational innovation: Short collaboration duration and low coauthor overlap predict individual innovation. These metrics could be transferred to study innovation in R&D teams or corporate labs. Rationale: Similar network structures (e.g., bridging ties, weak ties) are known to foster innovation in organizations. P1's specific metrics (duration, topic exposure, overlap) offer operationalizable measures for team composition studies.
    Evidence: Evidence 11 Evidence 23
  • C2: Mentorship effects on prizewinning (P5) and intellectual synthesis (P8) inform education policy: The finding that postdoctoral mentors and intellectual synthesis between mentors predict success can be applied to design interdisciplinary training programs in higher education. Rationale: Both papers identify specific mentorship features (mentor's prizewinning skill, intellectual synthesis) that boost protégé outcomes. These can be translated into training modules or mentor matching algorithms.
    Evidence: Evidence 27 Evidence 34 Evidence 35

5. Selected Hypothesis Portfolio

This section is the short answer: it presents the selected research-idea portfolio. By default the report keeps up to two hypotheses: a primary proposal and, when available, a distinct secondary or more incremental proposal. Each hypothesis is generated from the literature map and connected evidence, then scored for literature support, novelty, feasibility, cross-domain value, risk, obvious-extension penalty, and prior-work similarity. The primary hypothesis is used for novelty search and citation audit below. The secondary hypothesis is retained as a usable alternative, not as proof that it is equally novel or equally well supported.

Primary + high noveltyBest candidate for a new research direction, subject to novelty-search caveats.
Primary + high riskInteresting but fragile; prioritize feasibility and falsification before presenting it as a project.
Incremental secondaryUsable fallback or extension; expected to be less novel but often easier to test.
No evidence IDsIdea is mostly model inference; it should not be treated as source-supported.

Hypothesis generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned valid hypothesis candidates after binding and review.
Review: {'used': True, 'reason': 'LLM review returned replacement/refined hypotheses.', 'candidate_count': 2}

primary

H2 - The concentration of prizes among elites reduces the diversity of innovative ideas in science over time, as elite prizewinners' networks become insular and prioritize incremental work over radical novelty.

Total score: 0.495 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.8 Feasibility: 0.6 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.1

Why retained: Primary proposal selected for the main novelty search and citation audit. It is the strongest non-incremental candidate when available; otherwise it is the highest-scoring distinct candidate.

Motivation: P2 shows prize concentration, and P1 shows prizewinners are more innovative individually. However, if elites dominate, the overall field may suffer from reduced diversity. This tension is unresolved.

Novelty position: No prior study tests the long-term effect of prize concentration on idea diversity. This hypothesis connects two separate findings (P1, P2) into a testable claim.

How to test it: Compare the diversity of research topics (measured by cosine distance of paper keywords) in fields where prize concentration is high (top 10% of prizes won by few) vs. low (bottom 10%), using a panel of 20 years of publication data.

Premise evidence: Evidence 16 Evidence 17 Evidence 11

Supporting papers: P1: PNAS 2025 Vol. 122 No. 40 e2424143122 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significance P2: Scientific prize network predicts who pushes the boundaries of science

Risks:

  • Correlation may not imply causation; unobserved confounds (e.g., field maturity) could drive both concentration and diversity.
secondary

H1 - The innovation gap between prizewinners and nonprizewinners is driven by a selection effect: prizewinners possess pre-existing traits (e.g., risk-taking, cognitive flexibility) that cause both higher innovation and eventual prize receipt, rather than the prize itself causing sustained innovation.

Total score: 0.465 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.7 Feasibility: 0.5 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.2

Why retained: Retained as the next strongest distinct hypothesis because no clear incremental candidate was available.

Motivation: P1 shows that prizewinners become more innovative five years before the prize, and the gap persists after. This temporal pattern is consistent with selection on stable traits. P5 shows mentorship predicts prizewinning, suggesting traits may be transmitted. Testing causality is crucial for prize policy.

Novelty position: While P1 documents the timing, no prior work isolates selection vs. treatment. This hypothesis directly addresses the causal mechanism, which is a gap.

How to test it: Use a regression discontinuity design around prize eligibility thresholds (e.g., age or career stage limits) to compare innovation outcomes for winners just above vs. below the threshold. Alternatively, use a difference-in-differences approach comparing prizewinners to matched nonprizewinners before and after the prize, controlling for pre-trends.

Premise evidence: Evidence 6 Evidence 7 Evidence 27

Supporting papers: P1: PNAS 2025 Vol. 122 No. 40 e2424143122 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significance P5: Mentorship and protégé success in STEM fields

Risks:

  • Selection on unobservables may be hard to fully rule out; quasi-experimental designs may lack power.

6. Hypothesis Quality Audit

This section audits the generated idea itself, not just the references attached to it. The hypothesis is decomposed into components such as idea core, motivation, novelty position, mechanism, feasibility, experiment, and risks. Each component receives a small relevance-ranked evidence bundle when directly relevant evidence exists; otherwise it is labeled as model-inferred or needing review. This avoids padding the hypothesis with weak citations while still showing which parts are grounded in evidence IDs.

Source-stated + GREENThe component restates source text with provenance; it is evidence, not novelty.
Evidence-inspired + YELLOWThe component is motivated by evidence but goes beyond what papers directly prove.
Model-inferred + YELLOWThe component is a generated bridge or mechanism; it needs human review and experiments.
Unsupported / REDThe component lacks evidence, contradicts evidence, or makes an unsupported strong claim.

Summary: Hypothesis quality audit separates what the literature directly says from what the model inferred as a new idea. Generated idea, novelty, and experiment components should usually be YELLOW unless directly restating an established finding.

Components: 14; by status: {'needs_human_review': 7, 'evidence_inspired': 6, 'model_inferred': 1}; by color: {'yellow': 14}; precision evidence per component: None

Reviewers can inspect the text, source paper, location, and per-use support relation without chasing raw hash IDs through the appendix. We focus on understanding how the knowledge linkages among prizes and scientists’ propensities for prizewinning are related to knowledge pathways across disciplines and stratification within disciplines. Our data cover more than 3,000 different scientific prizes in diverse disciplines and the career histories of 10,455 prizewinners worldwide for more than 100 years. First, despite a proliferation of diverse prizes over time and across the globe, prizes are more concentrated within a relatively small group of scientific elites, and ties within the elites are more clustered, suggesting that a relatively constrained number of ideas and scholars lead science.

7. Hypothesis Novelty Search

This section searches for close prior work after the selected hypothesis is generated. After the primary hypothesis is selected, the system creates novelty-search queries from the hypothesis text, method terms, target problem, and supporting-paper neighborhood, then searches external literature again. The result is a risk assessment: exact/close matches suggest the idea may already exist, near matches suggest the idea needs sharper differentiation, related works provide background, and missing results only mean no close prior work was found by available tools. This section never proves novelty conclusively.

High riskExact or close prior work found; the idea likely needs substantial differentiation.
Medium riskNear work shares method, target problem, or framing; novelty may survive only with a sharper mechanism.
Low riskOnly background-related work found; still not proof of novelty.
Unknown riskSearch failed or evidence is too sparse; do not claim novelty.

Novelty risk: LOW

Conclusion: No close prior work was found by available tools, but related background work exists.

Reason: 6 related work(s) were found; absence of a close match is not proof of novelty.

8. Cited Reference Integrity Audit

This section audits only the references actually cited by the selected hypothesis atomic claims. It answers a bibliographic question: does the cited reference appear to exist, and do title, authors, year, DOI, venue, or source URL point to the same paper? It deliberately does not judge whether the reference supports the scientific claim; that is handled in the next section. Field status values separate matched/present metadata from missing or uncertain metadata. Missing DOI/year usually means the field was unavailable or not comparable, not automatically that the reference is wrong.

Score is bibliographic identity confidence, not a metadata-completeness percentage. A searched bibliographic record or local input PDF can score highly even when DOI or year is missing, but non-seed references with both DOI and year missing are downgraded to YELLOW.

GREEN integrityReference identity is verified enough for audit use; claim support is still judged separately.
YELLOW integrityReference probably exists but metadata is incomplete or uncertain; support cannot be upgraded to strong confidence.
RED integrityReference is not found or metadata conflicts strongly; related claim-support rows should be RED.
Missing DOI/yearUsually source coverage limitation, not a contradiction, unless other metadata also conflicts.

9. Claim Support Audit

This section audits whether each cited reference supports the specific atomic claim attached to it. Rows in this table are claim-reference pairs, so the same claim ID may appear more than once if multiple references are checked separately. This is stricter than bibliographic integrity: a real paper can be GREEN in the previous section but YELLOW or RED here if it only provides background, supports a weaker claim, discusses a different topic, or lacks usable evidence snippets.

Support type rule: direct means verified reference + explicit evidence for the specific claim; indirect means topic-relevant evidence that motivates but does not prove the claim; background means weak/abstract-only context; absent means no usable evidence or no valid reference.

Error category rule: no_specific_error_detected means this row was not classified as unsupported claim, overclaim, mis-citation, or contradiction. It does not mean the claim is proven; use Color, Support, Support Type, Evidence IDs, and Reason for the final interpretation.

GREEN + directVerified reference and explicit evidence directly support this exact claim.
YELLOW + indirect/backgroundEvidence motivates or contextualizes the claim but does not prove it.
RED + unsupported/absentNo usable evidence, invalid reference, contradiction, or clear unsupported claim.
no_specific_error_detectedNo four-category error was detected; this can still be YELLOW if support is indirect.

10. Evidence Cards

Evidence Cards expand evidence IDs into the human-readable form used throughout the report. Each card shows the source paper, URL or path, location, snippet text, and every claim or map item that reused the evidence. The same snippet can support multiple claims in different ways, so each use has its own support type, error category, and reason. Internal hash IDs are kept only in Appendix B for reproducibility; the numbered Evidence labels are intended for review.

One evidence, one useSimple case: one snippet is used for one claim or map item.
One evidence, multiple usesEach use has its own support type and reason; do not infer one global label for the snippet.
Abstract locationUseful but shallow; direct support from abstract alone should be interpreted cautiously.
PDF/full-text locationUsually stronger provenance because the snippet comes from a section or page-level source.

We focus on understanding how the knowledge linkages among prizes and scientists’ propensities for prizewinning are related to knowledge pathways across disciplines and stratification within disciplines. First, despite a proliferation of diverse prizes over time and across the globe, prizes are more concentrated within a relatively small group of scientific elites, and ties within the elites are more clustered, suggesting that a relatively constrained number of ideas and scholars lead science.

First, we derive the statistical properties of the global scientific prize network in terms of growth dynamics over time and the ensuing transition probabilities between prizes that can interlock subfields within a disciplines and fields between disciplines. The findings’ implications for innovation in science and the efficacy of reward systems and innovation in science are discussed.

Why: Uncertain or partial support: Evidence is relevant by content, but support is indirect or lexical-heavy.

First, despite a proliferation of diverse prizes over time and across the globe, prizes are more concentrated within a relatively small group of scientific elites, and ties within the elites are more clustered, suggesting that a relatively constrained number of ideas and scholars lead science. For example, 64.1% of prizewinners have won two prizes and 13.7% have one 5 or more prizes. Second, we find that certain prizes are strongly interlocked within and between disciplines by scientists who win multiple prizes, revealing the key pathways by which knowledge systematically gains credit and spreads through the network. Third, we find that genealogical and co-authorship networks strongly predict who wins one or more prizes and explains the high level of interconnectedness among acclaimed scientists and their path breaking ideas.

11. Controlled Citation Audit Cases

This section is a controlled capability demonstration, separate from the selected hypothesis. The system constructs one direct-support case, one uncertain or inferred case, and one deliberately wrong or nonexistent-reference case. These probes show whether the audit machinery can produce GREEN, YELLOW, and RED under known conditions. They should not be interpreted as extra scientific claims made by the hypothesis generator.

GREEN controlled caseKnown direct support case; should have evidence and pass as GREEN.
YELLOW controlled caseGenerated or inferred claim; should not be forced to GREEN.
RED controlled caseFake reference, contradiction, or unsupported absolute claim; should never become GREEN.
Failing controlled caseIndicates an audit rule regression rather than a scientific finding.

14. Memory Influence Summary

This section explains the role of persistent SQLite memory in the current run. Memory can store prior papers, references, hypotheses, evidence records, rejected evidence, and verification outcomes. Retrieval does not automatically become evidence: previous memory may influence query planning, flag duplicate-risk, or warn about previously rejected material, but direct verification is reused only when the current claim, reference, evidence, and rule version match. If memory was retrieved but did not materially affect the run, the report says so explicitly.

Retrieved + materially alteredMemory changed query planning, duplicate-risk assessment, or direct verification reuse.
Retrieved + not usedMemory was available but treated as context only; this is acceptable and should be reported honestly.
Duplicate risk highA similar previous hypothesis exists; novelty should be interpreted cautiously.
Negative memoryPreviously rejected snippets or noisy candidates are warning signals, never scientific counter-evidence by themselves.

Memory influenced query planning/context through same-scope paper hits or memory-derived queries.

Negative Memory

These are previous rejected evidence snippets or failed candidates. They are warning-only memories: useful for avoiding repeated retrieval mistakes, identifying noisy sources, or explaining why a candidate was ignored, but never evidence that a scientific claim is false. 15. Agent Reflection

This section is the agent's pre-report self-critique. The reflection tool checks whether the pipeline satisfied required ordering, whether retrieval found enough connected papers, whether novelty search ran, whether evidence is mostly abstract-level or full-text, whether reference integrity and claim support are credible, and whether memory materially influenced the result. Reflection findings are not hidden; they are reported as limitations or recommended next actions.

High severityMajor trust issue; rerun or inspect before using the result.
Medium severityInterpret with caution; usually evidence depth, sparse retrieval, or weak hypothesis score.
Low severityInformational limitation; useful for review but not necessarily blocking.
Passed checksPipeline invariants that succeeded, such as search-before-hypothesis or controlled audit behavior.

Summary: Reflection found no major structural problems, but the report remains limited by public API recall and available evidence depth.

16. Warnings and Limitations

This section collects automatic warnings and limitations detected during the run. Warnings are not automatic failures, but they mark places where a human reviewer should be cautious: shallow evidence, few retrieved papers, all-YELLOW claim support, high novelty risk, weak memory influence, API fallback, noisy reference extraction, or generated claims that remain mostly inferred.

No warningsThe automated checks found no obvious limitation, but human review is still required.
Evidence warningsSupport may be shallow, abstract-only, or missing full-text provenance.
Retrieval warningsFew connected papers, API fallback, or noisy search means the map may be incomplete.
Hypothesis warningsThe final idea may be mostly inferred, low-scoring, or duplicate-risk.
  • Evidence depth is limited: most evidence snippets come from abstracts rather than full text.
  • Some hypothesis components are model-inferred without direct evidence IDs; review the Hypothesis Quality Audit before treating them as conclusions.
  • Generated hypothesis support is mainly indirect, background, or absent.

Real API mode depends on public endpoints, available abstracts/full text, and external service coverage. Absence of retrieved close prior work is not proof of novelty.

Appendix A. Search and Retrieval Details

This appendix expands the retrieval pipeline. It shows the planned pre-generation queries, optional semantic-neighborhood expansion, kept papers, rejected papers, and the relatedness/citation-chain audit used to justify filtering. Kept papers are allowed into literature mapping and hypothesis generation; rejected papers are preserved for transparency but should not be treated as supporting evidence.

Kept paperPassed relatedness/citation-chain gate and can enter mapping or hypothesis generation.
Rejected paperRecorded for transparency but excluded from support and hypothesis generation.

Expansion disabledConnected pool was already sufficient or run mode did not require expansion.

8(E-3984bbd1ccbf)full_text_pdfinput_pdfPNAS 2025 Vol. 122 No. 40 e2424143122 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significancepdf:body:p10.9540 e2424143122 https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.2424143122 1 of 8 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significance Using a sample of thousands of science prizes worldwide, this study compares the career-wide innovativeness of prizewinners and a matched sample of similarly impactful nonprizewinner contenders. Scientific prizewinners are more likely to produce papers that combine existing ideas in new ways, join foundational and current research on a topic, and are more interdisciplinary than their matched nonprizewinning peers.
9(E-b84415860746)full_text_pdfinput_pdfPNAS 2025 Vol. 122 No. 40 e2424143122 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significancepdf:results:p40.953 , Panel B, presents margin plots based on the regression models. We observe that PWs and NPWs display overlapping levels of innovativeness during their formative career years, with PWs showing a gradually widening, significant research innovation gap over NPWs that first emerges roughly in the five years before the prize year, peaks at the prize year, and then persists at that level after the prize year. Additional specifications that control for PWs who win multiple prizes (SI Appendix, section 4.5.2 and Table S9 ) and include or omit review papers confirmed the main results (see SI Appendix, section 4.5.3, and Table S10 for details). Embeddedness and Innovation Prizewinners’ collaboration networks and innovativeness may be interrelated. The embeddedness of a researcher’s network has been found to be positively associated with innovation through access to information, trust-building, and a decrease in process losses.
10(E-454f65d31286)full_text_pdfinput_pdfPNAS 2025 Vol. 122 No. 40 e2424143122 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significancepdf:body:p10.95Analyzing 2,460 worldwide prizes, we compared the innovativeness of over 23,000 prizewinners and matched nonprizewinners whose performance records were statistically equivalent up to the prize year. We focus on understanding how the knowledge linkages among prizes and scientists’ propensities for prizewinning are related to knowledge pathways across disciplines and stratification within disciplines. Our data cover more than 3,000 different scientific prizes in diverse disciplines and the career histories of 10,455 prizewinners worldwide for more than 100 years. First, despite a proliferation of diverse prizes over time and across the globe, prizes are more concentrated within a relatively small group of scientific elites, and ties within the elites are more clustered, suggesting that a relatively constrained number of ideas and scholars lead science.

Our data cover more than 3,000 different scientific prizes in diverse disciplines and the career histories of 10,455 prizewinners worldwide for more than 100 years. First, despite a proliferation of diverse prizes over time and across the globe, prizes are more concentrated within a relatively small group of scientific elites, and ties within the elites are more clustered, suggesting that a relatively constrained number of ideas and scholars lead science. For example, 64.1% of prizewinners have won two prizes and 13.7% have one 5 or more prizes.
Evidence ID: 37 (E-5a5fc28571bf); Source: citation_graph; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: Scientific prize network predicts who pushes the boundaries of science; URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/1808.09412.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
Using comprehensive new data on prizes and prizewinners worldwide and across disciplines, we examine the growth dynamics and interlocking relationships found in the worldwide scientific prize network. We focus on understanding how the knowledge linkages among prizes and scientists’ propensities for prizewinning are related to knowledge pathways across disciplines and stratification within disciplines. Our data cover more than 3,000 different scientific prizes in diverse disciplines and the career histories of 10,455 prizewinners worldwide for more than 100 years.
Evidence ID: 38 (E-a895ce9949a0); Source: citation_graph; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: Scientific prize network predicts who pushes the boundaries of science; URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/1808.09412.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
First, despite a proliferation of diverse prizes over time and across the globe, prizes are more concentrated within a relatively small group of scientific elites, and ties within the elites are more clustered, suggesting that a relatively constrained number of ideas and scholars lead science. For example, 64.1% of prizewinners have won two prizes and 13.7% have one 5 or more prizes. Second, we find that certain prizes are strongly interlocked within and between disciplines by scientists who win multiple prizes, revealing the key pathways by which knowledge systematically gains credit and spreads through the network.
Evidence ID: 39 (E-cdbcce15ca91); Source: citation_graph; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: Scientific prize network predicts who pushes the boundaries of science; URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/1808.09412.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
The proliferation of prizes and links among prizes suggest that the prize network embodies information about scientists and ideas poised to grow in acclaim. Using comprehensive new data on prizes and prizewinners worldwide and across disciplines, we examine the growth dynamics and interlocking relationships found in the worldwide scientific prize network. We focus on understanding how the knowledge linkages among prizes and scientists’ propensities for prizewinning are related to knowledge pathways across disciplines and stratification within disciplines.
Evidence ID: 40 (E-a95ce2b39d89); Source: citation_graph; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: Scientific prize network predicts who pushes the boundaries of science; URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/1808.09412.pdf; Confidence: 0.916
First, we derive the statistical properties of the global scientific prize network in terms of growth dynamics over time and the ensuing transition probabilities between prizes that can interlock subfields within a disciplines and fields between disciplines. Second, we examine how genealogical ties and co-author ties are associated with prizewinning and the concentration of prizes among scientists. We demonstrate that despite a proliferation of diverse prizes over time and across the globe, prizes are more concentrated within a relatively small scientific elite, and ties within the elite are more clustered, suggesting that a relatively constrained number of ideas and scholars lead science.
Evidence ID: 41 (E-124aae5f1e6d); Source: citation_graph; Location: pdf:introduction:p2; Paper: Scientific prize network predicts who pushes the boundaries of science; URL: https://arxiv.org/pdf/1808.09412.pdf; Confidence: 0.909

CL3 - YELLOW

Their research is more likely to combine existing ideas in new ways, integrate a topic’s historical and contemporary thinking, and incorporate interdisciplinary perspectives. Second, although prizewinners and matched nonprizewinners have statistically equivalent impact and productivity records up to the prize year, at about five years before the prize, prizewinners’ papers become more innovative than their matched peers. This difference widens each year, peaks during the prize year, and then persists for the remainder of their careers.
Evidence ID: 43 (E-e723f31e4ac3); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:body:p1; Paper: PNAS 2025 Vol. 122 No. 40 e2424143122 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significance; URL: D:\WeChat Files\wxid_6nut6ell2yad22\FileStorage\File\2026-06\tian-et-al-2025-the-distinctive-innovation-patterns-and-network-embeddedness-of-scientific-prizewinners(1).pdf; Confidence: 0.95
Yet in practice, prizes may inadvertently divert resources from similarly impactful but less celebrated scholars. Analyzing 2,460 worldwide prizes, we compared the innovativeness of over 23,000 prizewinners and matched nonprizewinners whose performance records were statistically equivalent up to the prize year. Their research is more likely to combine existing ideas in new ways, integrate a topic’s historical and contemporary thinking, and incorporate interdisciplinary perspectives.
Evidence ID: 44 (E-c17475a4de67); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:body:p1; Paper: PNAS 2025 Vol. 122 No. 40 e2424143122 RESEARCH ARTICLE | Significance; URL: D:\WeChat Files\wxid_6nut6ell2yad22\FileStorage\File\2026-06\tian-et-al-2025-the-distinctive-innovation-patterns-and-network-embeddedness-of-scientific-prizewinners(1).pdf; Confidence: 0.95
This difference widens each year, peaks during the prize year, and then persists for the remainder of their careers. Compared to nonprizewinners, prizewinners’ collaborations are shorter in duration, encompass wider exposure to unfamiliar topics, and involve coauthors whose networks minimally overlap with each other. The findings’ implications for innovation in science and the efficacy of reward systems and innovation in science are discussed.
